            }
            return "blank";
        }

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="ee10b0ca4a06436094fde3563ccd6045">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '42f83b42-948d-49fe-ab7a-209dba6f57f5'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Yang/Jia%E2%80%90Lin">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Zheng/Shuo%E2%80%90Hang" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Shuo-Hang Zheng</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">
                     <p class="author-name">Shuo-Hang Zheng</p>
                     <p>MOE Key Laboratory for UV Light-Emitting Materials and Technology, Northeast Normal University, Changchun, Jilin, 130024 P. R. China</p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Zheng/Shuo%E2%80%90Hang">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Wu/Xing%E2%80%90Long" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a9" data-db-target-for="a9" aria-controls="a9" aria-haspopup="true" id="a9_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Xing-Long Wu<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a9" aria-labelledby="a9_Ctrl" role="region" id="a9">
                     <p class="author-type mb-1">Corresponding Author</p>
                     <p class="author-name">Xing-Long Wu</p>
                     <ul class="rlist sm-account">
                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a class="sm-account__link" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:xinglong@nenu.edu.cn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-ic_mail_solid"></i><span>xinglong@nenu.edu.cn</span></a></li>

                     </ul>
                     <p>MOE Key Laboratory for UV Light-Emitting Materials and Technology, Northeast Normal University, Changchun, Jilin, 130024 P. R. China</p>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d145083715" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Sodium (Na) super-ionic conductor structured Na<sub>3</sub>MnTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NMTP) cathodes have garnered interest owing to their cost-effectiveness and high operating voltages. However, the voltage hysteresis phenomenon triggered by <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f9ddb226-00bc-4df4-b0d5-096d3cecb27a/adma202410797-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0002.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msubsup>
                     <mi>Mn</mi>
                     <mi>Na</mi>
                     <mo>·</mo>
                  </msubsup>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{Mn}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}}^{\mathrm{\cdot}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> anti-site defects (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d9561456-239d-4298-8f1a-32af9c94e4b7/adma202410797-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0003.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msubsup>
                     <mi>Mn</mi>
                     <mi>Na</mi>
                     <mo>·</mo>
                  </msubsup>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{Mn}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}}^{\mathrm{\cdot}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>-ASD), namely, the occupation of Mn<sup>2+</sup> in the Na2 vacancies in NMTP, leads to sluggish diffusion kinetics and low energy efficiency. This study employs an innovative electronic confinement-restrained strategy to achieve the regulation of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f8341cf7-18a3-48d0-bb04-450f7f5a5f2c/adma202410797-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0004" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0004.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msubsup>
                     <mi>Mn</mi>
                     <mi>Na</mi>
                     <mo>·</mo>
                  </msubsup>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{Mn}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}}^{\mathrm{\cdot}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>-ASD. Partial replacement of titanium (Ti) with electron-rich vanadium (V) favors strong electronic interactions with Mn<sup>2+</sup>, restraining Mn<sup>2+</sup> migration. The results suggest that this strategy can significantly increase the vacancy formation energy and migration energy barrier of manganese (Mn), thus inhibiting <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/4d9bea63-9561-46ae-9df8-8a21e546ce63/adma202410797-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0005" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0005.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msubsup>
                     <mi>Mn</mi>
                     <mi>Na</mi>
                     <mo>·</mo>
                  </msubsup>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{Mn}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}}^{\mathrm{\cdot}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>-ASD formation. As proof of this concept, an Na-rich Na<sub>3.5</sub>MnTi<sub>0.5</sub>V<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NMTVP) material is designed, wherein the electronic interaction enhanced the redox activity and achieved more Na<sup>+</sup> storage under high-voltage. The NMTVP cathode delivered a reversible specific capacity of up to 182.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and output an excellent specific energy of 513.8&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to ≈3.2 electron transfer processes, wherein the energy efficiency increased by 35.5% at 30&nbsp;C. Through the confinement effect of electron interactions, this strategy provides novel perspectives for the exploitation and breakthrough of high-energy-density cathode materials in Na-ion batteries.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410797-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410797-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Electrochemical energy storage drives the global trend toward green energy and a low-carbon economy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0001_R_d145083698e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun. Nat. Chem. Science Nat. Energy.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As competitive energy-storage candidates with great potential, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have garnered considerably attention by many scholars and have been rapidly developed in the past decade owing to their abundant resources, excellent sustainability, and low cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0002_R_d145083698e498" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Energy. Nat. Nanotechnol.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, the larger mass and radius of sodium (Na) pose various challenges, such as slow ion diffusion kinetics, unsatisfactory cycle life, and low energy/power density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0003_R_d145083698e503" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Currently, most studies on improving the performance of SIBs have focused on the design of advanced materials or the construction of intricate, multilevel porous nanostructures, which are expected to achieve high energy/power density and long-term cycling life.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0004_R_d145083698e509" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Prog. Mater Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In particular, cathode materials are the forefront of SIB investigations and have considerably determined the electrochemical performance of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0005_R_d145083698e514" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, cathode materials with fast kinetics and high energy densities must be developed to meet this increasing demand. Currently, a range of cathode materials such as layered transition metals (TMs) oxides,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0006_R_d145083698e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nat. Commun. Nano Energy">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> polyanionic compounds (PACs),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0007_R_d145083698e525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Prussian blue analogs,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0008_R_d145083698e530" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Commun.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> are utilized for Na storage. Among these materials, PACs with easily adjustable redox potentials and impressive structural robustness have garnered considerable attention.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0009_R_d145083698e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Na-super-ionic conductor (NASICON) as the typical PACs with excellent structural stability, high safety and good thermal stability are regarded as ideal SIBs cathode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0010_R_d145083698e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nat. Commun.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The general formula of the structural unit of NASICON is Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>M<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (M represents TMs), with a 3D open-framework configuration formed by a corner-sharing PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedron and MO<sub>6</sub> octahedron, which promotes the convenient transport of Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0011_R_d145083698e563" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There are two independent Na<sup>+</sup> sites, namely, (Na1, 6b) and (Na2, 18e), with different coordination environments of Na<sup>+</sup>, and Na<sup>+</sup> ions are inclined to be reversibly extracted from and inserted into the Na2 sites during the charge/discharge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0004_R_d145083698e574" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Prog. Mater Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, numerous manganese (Mn)-based NASICON cathode materials have been designed and synthesized owing to the cost-effectiveness and high operating voltage, such as Na<sub>3</sub>MnTi(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NMTP),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0012_R_d145083698e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Energy.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na<sub>4</sub>MnV(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NMVP)<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0013_R_d145083698e598" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Na<sub>3</sub>MnZr(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>(NMZP),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0014_R_d145083698e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and developed as the high-performance cathode in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0015_R_d145083698e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Storage Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Among the aforementioned Mn-based NASICON cathode materials, NMTP has garnered considerable attention owing to its excellent theoretical specific capacity of 176 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> by the three-electron transfer process within the voltage range of 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V, associated with the redox reactions of Mn<sup>4+/3+</sup> (≈4.0&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> (≈3.6&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), and Ti<sup>4+/3+</sup> (≈2.1&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0016_R_d145083698e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Energy Storage Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the poor electronic conductivity, sluggish diffusion kinetics, and inferior energy efficiency restricts its practical applications, attributed to the low mobility of carriers and undesirable voltage hysteresis by <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/5bf6cce8-77c0-4bc9-adc5-13557756befe/adma202410797-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0006" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0006.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msubsup>
                     <mi>Mn</mi>
                     <mi>Na</mi>
                     <mo>·</mo>
                  </msubsup>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{Mn}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}}^{\mathrm{\cdot}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> anti-site defects (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/aaa33bd6-9d88-48df-b98c-e70785ecada8/adma202410797-math-0007.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0007" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0007.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msubsup>
                     <mi>Mn</mi>
                     <mi>Na</mi>
                     <mo>·</mo>
                  </msubsup>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{Mn}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}}^{\mathrm{\cdot}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>-ASD).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0017_R_d145083698e661" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano Energy">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Owing to these limitations, scholars have improved the carrier mobility of materials through carbon coating, elemental doping, and porous structure designs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0018_R_d145083698e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Energy Storage Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the formation and regulation of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/875885ea-378d-4a7e-8a1d-d5a551e25281/adma202410797-math-0008.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0008" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0008.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msubsup>
                     <mi>Mn</mi>
                     <mi>Na</mi>
                     <mo>·</mo>
                  </msubsup>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{Mn}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}}^{\mathrm{\cdot}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>-ASD in NMTP has scarcely been investigated. Closely related to Mn<sup>2+</sup> migration and occupation of the Na2 site, the voltage hysteresis phenomenon is an obvious additional voltage plateau (charged and discharged to ≈4 and 2.6&nbsp;V, respectively) in the galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD) curves, further increasing the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion barrier and inhibiting the corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0012_R_d145083698e687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Energy.">12</a>, <a href="#adma202410797-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0017_R_d145083698e687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano Energy">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the Mn-related reversible high-voltage capacity is sacrificed in the NMTP cathode, leading to low energy density and efficiency and weakening its competitiveness. Recently, cutting-edge explorations of NMTP have been shown to suppress the occupation of Na sites by Mn by doping with molybdenum (Mo) and excess Na to increase the formation energy of Mn<sup>2+</sup> occupation for Na2.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0012_R_d145083698e695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Energy.">12</a>, <a href="#adma202410797-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0017_R_d145083698e695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano Energy">17</a>, <a href="#adma202410797-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0019_R_d145083698e695" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Energy Lett.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The introduction of Mo optimizes the voltage hysteresis but at the expense of reducing the redox activity in NMTP. Balancing these two factors remains a significant challenge.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we innovatively utilized the electronic confinement strategy in an NMTP lattice, restraining the <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/859a0ffc-32d8-442c-ab04-69adb03ae3e3/adma202410797-math-0009.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0009" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0009.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msubsup>
                     <mi>Mn</mi>
                     <mi>Na</mi>
                     <mo>·</mo>
                  </msubsup>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{Mn}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}}^{\mathrm{\cdot}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>-ASD, while also improving the electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0020_R_d145083698e712" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By partially substituting titanium (Ti) with electron-rich vanadium (V), the enhanced electronic interactions between V<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>2+</sup> restrain Mn<sup>2+</sup> migration. Additionally, the electron-rich substitution of V synergistically enhanced the redox activity and average voltage of NMTP, thereby boosting its energy density. Notably, the vacancy formation energy and migration energy barrier of Mn increased following the substitution of V, eliminating the adverse effects of lattice defects caused by Mn migration. Based on the realization of greater Na<sup>+</sup> storage at a high-voltage, Na<sub>3.5</sub>MnTi<sub>0.5</sub>V<sub>0.5</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NMTVP) achieves redox reactions of ≈3.2 electrons transfer processes and delivers a reversible capacity of 182.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Electronic confinement also stabilizes the crystal structure during the electrochemical cycling process. Furthermore, the NMTVP cathode demonstrated exceptional rate capability, delivering a storage capacity of 93.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at 30 C. The attainable peak energy density of 513.8&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and energy efficiency (EE) of up to 89.5% at 0.5 C were ascribed to the elimination of voltage hysteresis. Electron confinement helps exploits electron interactions and successfully restrains defects, taking advantage of the competitiveness of Mn-rich NASICON electrodes in SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202410797-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202410797-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410797-fig-0001">1a</a> shows the GCD curve test of NMTP within a testing voltage range of 1.5 to 4.3&nbsp;V. Notably, NMTP has a discharge-specific capacity of 162.4&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 C, and an additional voltage plateau is observed within the voltage range of 2.3–2.6&nbsp;V. This phenomenon was primarily attributed to the voltage hysteresis caused by defects in NMTP itself. Additionally, similar electrochemical behavior was observed during the charging process (≈4.0&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). The additional voltage plateaus indicate the formation of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/19c8563d-d7ff-46bf-83f1-8fe03455eebd/adma202410797-math-0010.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0010" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0010.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msubsup>
                     <mi>Mn</mi>
                     <mi>Na</mi>
                     <mo>·</mo>
                  </msubsup>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{Mn}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}}^{\mathrm{\cdot}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>-ASD, consistent with the literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0012_R_d145083698e771" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Nat. Energy.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> More importantly, it suppresses the redox activity of Mn<sup>2+</sup><sup>/</sup><sup>3+</sup> with reduces the capacity contribution at the plateau, thereby lowering the energy density contribution of NMTP itself and decreasing its EE. Moreover, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0001">1b</a> shows the EE of NMTP from 0.1 to 30 C, which decays to only 56.8% at 30 C, and its average energy efficiency (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/5db444d6-97f7-4595-ae25-028df1210187/adma202410797-math-0011.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0011" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0011.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mover accent="true">
                     <mrow>
                        <mi>E</mi>
                        <mi>E</mi>
                     </mrow>
                     <mo>¯</mo>
                  </mover>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar {EE}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>) is 75.14%. Subsequently, the calculated variance (σ<sup>2</sup>) indicates the stability of its EE as can be seen it fluctuates drastically (σ<sup>2</sup> = 41.27). Therefore, the effect of inherent defects in NMTP on its electrochemical behavior underscores the importance of defect management in optimizing the performance of electrode materials. By addressing these limitations, the capacity and EE of NMTP can be enhanced, making it a more viable option for use in SIBs.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202410797-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f396010a-4dac-4c68-b7ab-2fff017596f7/adma202410797-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f396010a-4dac-4c68-b7ab-2fff017596f7/adma202410797-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2f43a7a1-ac41-4312-8be7-ad779d8f08ed/adma202410797-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f396010a-4dac-4c68-b7ab-2fff017596f7/adma202410797-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410797-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410797" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) GCD curves of NMTP at 0.1 C. b) EE of NMTP at 0.1–30 C.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>As proof-of-concept, NMTVP was designed through V substitution. Rietveld refinement of X-ray diffraction (XRD) was used to analyze the structural characteristics and phase purity of the crystals. As shown in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#adma202410797-fig-0002">2a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a> (Supporting Information), NMTP and NMTVP exhibited similar diffraction peaks, indicating the well-defined crystallinity of the typical NASICON structure. The Rietveld refinement results showed that NMTVP exhibited a trigonal (<i>R-</i>3<i>c</i>) structure and verified the complete integration of the TM ions into the framework. Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) present the crystal cell parameters of NMTP and NMTVP, confirming substitution at the Ti site. The cell parameter a = b of NMTP is 8.79200 Å, clearly less than that (8.85872 Å) of NMTVP. Additionally, the reduction in the c-axis distance indicates that V was embedded in the lattice. The unit cell volumes of NMTP (1455.95943 Å<sup>3</sup>) and NMTVP (1477.631184 Å<sup>3</sup>) indicated that the substitution of V induced the desired volume expansion in the lattice structure, implying that NMTVP was more conducive to the migration of Na<sup>+</sup>. The crystal structure of NMTVP featured a 3D open framework comprising MO<sub>6</sub> octahedra (where M represents Mn, V, or Ti) and PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra interconnected through shared angular O atoms (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, two independent Na<sup>+</sup> sites, namely, (Na1, 6b) and (Na2, 18e), originate from different coordination environments. However, only Na2 is considered to be electrochemically active.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0003_R_d145083698e856" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Soc. Rev. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Na2 active occupancy sites increased and vacancies decreased in NMTVP, thereby inhibiting the formation of defects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0017_R_d145083698e862" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano Energy">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Both materials exhibited similar diffraction peaks and high crystallinity, which could be accurately indexed to the NASICON-structured NMTP phase (PDF #53-0018) shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information). Moreover, two broad and distinct bands were observed for NMTP and NMTVP, centered at ≈1326 and 1592 cm<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to disordered carbon (D band) and graphitic sp<sup>2</sup> carbon (G band), respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). The presence of pyrolytic carbon with partial graphitization, as evidenced by the intensity ratio of the D- to G-bands of ≈0.91 in NMTVP, suggests a more favorable electronic conductivity than that of NMTP. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) shows the thermogravimetry (TG) curves of NMTVP and NMTP, which were used to determine the carbon content. The TG curves reveal that carbon combustion at 270–800&nbsp;°C for NMTVP and NMTP results in mass losses of 4.31 and 11.22&nbsp;wt.%, respectively. The slight mass loss below 270&nbsp;°C is attributed to the water loss. The decrease in the carbon content of NMTVP may be ascribed to the reduction of organic Ti raw materials during the synthesis process and consumption of the V reduction reaction during the annealing process.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202410797-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/843d3c2f-fc31-4aad-a302-418a69a97a8a/adma202410797-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/843d3c2f-fc31-4aad-a302-418a69a97a8a/adma202410797-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b223aedb-61d8-4965-9e70-e0078ec5335f/adma202410797-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/843d3c2f-fc31-4aad-a302-418a69a97a8a/adma202410797-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202410797-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202410797" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD Rietveld refinement results of NMTVP. b) HRTEM image with the FFT image and line profile on the top and bottom right images, respectively. c) Mn vacancy formation energy of NMTP and NMTVP, and d) schematic illustration for the difference charge density.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), the surface elemental composition and valence states of NMTVP and NMTP were also probed through X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). The signals were clearly distinguishable for each element in the high-resolution XPS spectra of NMTVP and NMTP, demonstrating that V existed in the NMTVP lattice by substituting Ti. Notably, two peaks at 654.5 and 642.5&nbsp;eV were assigned to the electrons in Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 2p<sub>3/2</sub> for Mn<sup>3+</sup>, respectively, and another pair of peaks at 653.4 and 641.1&nbsp;eV corresponds to the electrons in 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and 2p<sub>3/2</sub> for Mn<sup>2+</sup> in the Mn 2p spectrum, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0021_R_d145083698e922" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - ACS Energy Lett. Small">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The sharp peaks in the 2p spectrum of V centered at 517.02 (V 2p<sub>3/2</sub>) and 523.58&nbsp;eV (V 2p<sub>1/2</sub>) are characteristic of V<sup>3+</sup> species (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0022_R_d145083698e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Energy Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the 2p spectrum of Ti was fitted and divided into Ti<sup>3+</sup> and Ti<sup>4+</sup>: the characteristic peaks observed at 467.0 and 460.23&nbsp;eV correspond to Ti<sup>4+</sup> species; those at 465.74 and 459.8&nbsp;eV correspond to Ti<sup>3+</sup> species, demonstrating the coexistence of Ti in multiple valence states in NMTVP (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6c</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202410797-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202410797-bib-0011_R_d145083698e954" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Simultaneously, similar to those in NMTVP, Mn, and Ti exist in NMTP in mixed valence states (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the embedding of V does not affect the valence state of TMs in NMTP. The morphological features of NMTVP and NMTP were analyzed by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), the SEM images reveal that both were formed by the agglomeration of many irregular nanoscale primary particles. The TEM images shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information) indicate that their particle size is ≈800–1500&nbsp;nm, and that they are confined in the continuous carbon matrix. Furthermore, as shown in the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image of NMTVP (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0002">2b</a>), a carbon layer coated the particle with an average thickness of 5–7&nbsp;nm. The distinct lattice fringe spacing of 0.32&nbsp;nm corresponds to the (024) plane of NMTVP, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0002">2b</a> (bottom right). The clear lattice fringes corresponding to the (113) crystallographic planes of NMTP were also observed with spacings of 0.37&nbsp;nm (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the fast Fourier transform (FFT, top right in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0002">2b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information) mode confirmed the high crystallinity of both samples. We inferred that the substitution of V for the Ti sites in the lattice did not adversely affect the crystallinity of the material. The elemental energy-dispersive X-ray (EDX) mapping by SEM clearly revealed that all elements were uniformly distributed throughout the as-prepared NMTVP and NMTP particles (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), indicating successful V substitution. The results of inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectrometry (ICP-AES) are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information shown. The Na:Mn:V:Ti:P atomic ratio was 3.5:1:0.5:0.5:3, confirming that the specific atomic ratio in the NMTVP cathode corresponds well with the experimental design. Furthermore, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information, compared with that in NMTP (2.26244E<sup>−10</sup>-3.32779E<sup>−10</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>), the embedding of V in NMTVP (6.19771E<sup>−7</sup>-7.08215E<sup>−7</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>) improves the conductivity of the material and accelerates electron transport.</p>
         
         <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were then conducted to confirm that electron confinement-restrained strategy can efficiently inhibit the formation of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/dae93aa8-02f8-4cc7-8eb4-1303522e134d/adma202410797-math-0012.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0012" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0012.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msubsup>
                     <mi>Mn</mi>
                     <mi>Na</mi>
                     <mo>·</mo>
                  </msubsup>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{Mn}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}}^{\mathrm{\cdot}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>-ASD in NMTVP, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0002">2c</a>, the results indicate that the vacancy formation energy of the Mn atoms increased from 1.32 to 2.46&nbsp;eV owing to the enhanced electronic interaction between Mn and V. Moreover, the difference in charge density (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0002">2d</a>) shows that owing to the abundant electron transfer of the doped V with the coordination environment, which enhances the stability of the structure, the migration process of Mn to the Na site is impeded. Notably, the substitution of V may strengthen the electronic interactions between V atoms and the surrounding coordination environment. Strong electronic interactions typically indicate the presence of stronger chemical bonds or interactions, as V possesses more electrons and more electron shells than Ti. Furthermore, the enhanced electronic interactions correspond to the formation of chemical bonds, resulting in a higher binding energy between TM ions. We inferred that the electronic interaction between V<sup>3+</sup> and Mn<sup>2+</sup> may be enhanced, and that the confined electron densities also increase in the interaction region. Therefore, the electron-confinement-restrained strategy successfully limited the migration of Mn to achieve defect regulation.</p>
         
         <p>The impact of the electronic confinement-restrained strategy on the electrochemical behavior of NMTVP was evaluated by investigating the GCD curves and cyclic voltammetry (CV) at a potential range of 1.5–4.3&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) with coin-type cells. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410797-fig-0003">3a</a>, the NMTP delivered an initial discharge-specific capacity of 155.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and exhibited a typical voltage hysteresis characteristic curve. Correspondingly, NMTVP exhibits markedly disparate electrochemical behavior that delivers a high specific capacity of 182.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with ≈3.2 electron transfer processes, and the additional voltage plateaus disappear, indicating the effective elimination of voltage hysteresis by regulating <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/9a017194-c154-460c-98f0-4628a5cdd4e7/adma202410797-math-0013.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0013" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0013.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msubsup>
                     <mi>Mn</mi>
                     <mi>Na</mi>
                     <mo>·</mo>
                  </msubsup>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{Mn}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}}^{\mathrm{\cdot}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>-ASD. The entire GCD curve profiles are divided into three voltage regions, namely, I (3.7–4.3&nbsp;V), II (2.7–3.7&nbsp;V), and III (1.5–2.7&nbsp;V). The discharge-specific capacities calculated from the discharge curves are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0003">3b</a>. NMTVP delivered discharge-specific capacities of 21.9, 76.9, and 83.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in regions I, II, and III, respectively. In particular, the specific discharge capacity of NMTVP increased from 33.0 to 76.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in region II owing to the activation of the Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> reduction reaction, and the proportion of high-voltage specific discharge capacity increased from 22.0 to 54.1% during the discharge process. Therefore, the electron confinement-restrained strategy of restraining defects enabled the NMTVP cathode to obtain a higher working voltage and discharge specific capacity, thereby increasing the specific energy. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0003">3c</a>, in the CV curve of the NMTVP cathode, three pairs of redox peaks were observed at ≈2.25, 3.58, and 4.11&nbsp;V, corresponding to the reactions of Ti<sup>3+/4+</sup>, V<sup>3+/4+</sup> and Mn<sup>2+/3+</sup>, and Mn<sup>3+/4+</sup> and V<sup>4+/5+</sup> (V<sup>5+/4+</sup> and V<sup>3+/4+</sup> overlapping with Mn<sup>3+/4+</sup> and Mn<sup>2+/3+</sup>redox peaks), respectively, which coincide with the GCD curves. The redox peak at ≈3.58&nbsp;V was sharper and more symmetrical for NMTVP than for NMTP, indicating improved Mn<sup>2+/3+</sup> reaction kinetics and smaller polarization. The reduction peak of NMTP exhibited a bulge at ≈2.6&nbsp;V, which corresponded to voltage hysteresis of the GCD curve. Moreover, the enhanced electronic interactions are generally associated with a larger TM element migration energy barrier, which accounts for the atomic stability in the crystal structure. The migration path of Mn to the Na sites is depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0003">3d</a>. The Mn migration barrier of NMTVP (0.48&nbsp;eV) was higher than that of NMTP (0.228&nbsp;eV), as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0003">3e</a>, indicating that more energy is required to break electronic interactions between V and Mn for the formation of <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/304cc080-b98b-4a78-a451-e5b03b321b81/adma202410797-math-0014.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0014" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0014.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msubsup>
                     <mi>Mn</mi>
                     <mi>Na</mi>
                     <mo>·</mo>
                  </msubsup>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{Mn}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}}^{\mathrm{\cdot}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>-ASD, implying that NMTVP has a more stable structure.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adma202410797-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8bf85069-fd43-4dde-81db-b299c3c09c6e/adma202410797-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8bf85069-fd43-4dde-81db-b299c3c09c6e/adma202410797-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c40e6060-8da9-425e-964e-3a921b09f22a/adma202410797-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8bf85069-fd43-4dde-81db-b299c3c09c6e/adma202410797-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

               </figcaption>

         </section>
         <p>NMTVP and NMTP cathodes were tested under a continuously varying rate from 0.1 to 30 C to further analyze the impact of the electron confinement-restrained strategy on their electrochemical properties, as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adma202410797-fig-0004">4a–c</a>. We concluded that the apparent improved rate capability of the NMTVP cathode was achieved by regulating <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/26dc08cd-3c1e-450b-862f-3366b6c6e897/adma202410797-math-0015.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0015" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0015.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msubsup>
                     <mi>Mn</mi>
                     <mi>Na</mi>
                     <mo>·</mo>
                  </msubsup>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{Mn}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}}^{\mathrm{\cdot}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>-ASD, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0004">4a</a>, delivering storage capacities of 182.7, 139, 127.3, 120.4, 112.7, 107.8, 107.2, 103.3, 99.6, and 94.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> as the current increased from 0.1 to 30 C, respectively. Notably, the NMTVP cathode recovered its initial capacity after the current was returned to 0.1 C. The GCD curves are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0004">4b,c</a>, wherein the high-voltage plateau disappeared, and the voltage hysteresis phenomenon became increasingly significant as the current density increased, whereas the more stable reversible de/insertion process of Na<sup>+</sup> in NMTVP ensured better voltage plateau retention. The sawtooth pattern at high rates can be attributed to an insufficient number of recorded points. Simultaneously, the cycling performances of the NMTVP and NMTP cathodes are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0004">4d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). The capacity retention of NMTVP reached 81.2% after 1200 cycles at 5 C, which was higher than that of NMTP (68.5%). Notably, NMTVP showed a storage capacity of 96.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> even at 10 C, maintaining 92.3% of capacity retention after 1000 cycles; thus, the excellent long-term cycling stability and crystal structure was well-sustained by restraining <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/86d48d3c-223e-429c-a01f-9b1eb2dc9103/adma202410797-math-0016.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0016" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0016.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msubsup>
                     <mi>Mn</mi>
                     <mi>Na</mi>
                     <mo>·</mo>
                  </msubsup>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{Mn}}_{{\mathrm{Na}}}^{\mathrm{\cdot}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>-ASD. Notably, the electrode was not fully activated at a high rate of 10 C in the cycle performance test, which may lead to a slight difference in capacity. The <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/97c829c0-d37a-46cb-8a00-de56fbbc4fc6/adma202410797-math-0017.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0017" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0017.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mover accent="true">
                        <mi>E</mi>
                        <mo>¯</mo>
                     </mover>
                     <mi>E</mi>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{\bar{E}}E}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> of the samples at different rates are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0004">4e</a>. Notably, the EE of NMTVP is higher than that of NMTP at all rates, exhibiting an overall trend wherein the effect becomes more obvious with an increase in the rate. This proves that the electronic confinement-restrained strategy greatly improves the EE of NMTP and reduces the energy loss during electrochemical processes. The EE of NMTVP reached a peak of 89.5% at 0.5 C and remained as high as 77.0% even at 30 C, which was markedly enhanced compared with that of NMTP (56.8%); thus, the EE of NMTP was enhanced by 35.5 percentage points (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). Notably, the smaller fluctuation amplitude (σ<sup>2</sup> = 14.56) indicated that the stability and homogeneity of the EE are guaranteed, even for conversions at different current densities, further favoring its commercial application. The comparison of NMTP and NMTVP in 5D (energy density, specifical capacity, power density, average voltage, and polarization (-ΔV)) according to the electrochemical performance and calculation results is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0004">4f</a> and presented in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information. We concluded that owing to the elimination of voltage hysteresis through electron confinement, the electrochemical performance of the NMTVP cathode is optimized in all aspects, manifesting great potential for applications in SIBs. The electrode kinetics of NMTP and NMTVP were investigated using CV at various scan rates and the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT). The dynamic properties of Na<sup>+</sup> de/insertion were relevant to the peak current evolution of the CV curves. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a> (Supporting Information) shows the CV at various scan rates (0.1–1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>); the recording potential was set at a range from 1.5 to 4.3&nbsp;V (versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). The peak currents (<i>I<sub>p</sub></i>) of the redox peaks of both samples increased with increasing scan rate, corresponding to a fast reaction rate. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information) shows the well-fitted linear relationship between <i>I<sub>p</sub></i> and the square root of the scan rate (<i>ν</i><sup>1/2</sup>), suggesting that the redox processes are diffusion-controlled. Moreover, the apparent diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>D<sub>app,Na</sub></i>) in the samples during the redox reaction was derived using the Randles-Sevcik equation (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0004">4g</a>). The results indicated that the <i>D<sub>app,Na</sub></i> value of NMTVP in the B/B’ region was considerably higher than that of NMTP, which is attributed to the improvement in the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics relative to Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> redox activity. Similarly, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202410797-fig-0004">4h</a>, GITT was used to further compare the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion ability. NMTVP exhibited a higher <i>D<sub>app,Na</sub></i> value (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/32374ae9-d88c-4c41-b2ee-285f9c20dfbf/adma202410797-math-0018.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0018" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0018.png">

Moreover, for the NMTP cathode, the sluggish diffusion behavior at 2.6&nbsp;V triggered by <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/d02aeffa-9c57-420b-b672-7fe5303ca23f/adma202410797-math-0020.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202410797:adma202410797-math-0020" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202410797-math-0020.png">
